###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT Science versus religion: Is compromise impossible?

_(reply depth: 35)_

- **From:** Robert Wagner <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net>
- **Date:** 2005-01-13T07:24:37+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<2n4cu0h41qioj6evo43lspu9eppjfe584o@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<crf3ek$24a8$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <gu4nt09hir9otrgl027q10m3rsrij8ghm8@4ax.com> <crh9vg$ig4$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <6hjot01ra1o0lhl79ba6fap5coh99qv2j5@4ax.com> <crkium$2pvv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <cggtt0pel8lt3hn2hmp0ei49l5idloqdvq@4ax.com> <crmrqe$19pe$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <jguut0paokdl3eb0t1bncffn1254pc2eri@4ax.com> <crupc5$ifd$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <peu5u01re3fascebdunqchrcdn37ushvsu@4ax.com> <cs12m1$24is$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`

```
On Tue, 11 Jan 2005 09:34:18 -0800, "Chuck Stevens"
<charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:

>
>"Robert Wagner" <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
>news:peu5u01re3fascebdunqchrcdn37ushvsu@4ax.com...


>> No, I still don't see it. Article 1.8.11 doesn't grant any rights to
>> US citizens. It assumes privateers will be available, primarily from
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
>Federal law expressly allows him to do?  That one's new to me ...

Let's suppose the Cobol Standard was the US Constitution. In this
parable:
Cobol = The Feds
Operating Systems = States
Programmers = citizens
Daemons = privateers
Device drivers = military
Memory access = guns
IO port access = cannons
LPARs = other countries

The Founding Fathers, coming from assembly language backgrounds, wrote
in Article 1.8.11 'Cobol  shall have the authority to call daemons.'
It doesn't say one way or the other whether daemons can be written in
Cobol, it just assumes they'll always be available. 

Programmers, fearing a despotic Cobol, insist on a Second Amendment
reading 'Unfettered memory access being essential to programming,
neither Cobol nor operating systems shall limit a program's access to
the machine's memory.'

Years later daemons become obsolete. They are replaced by services and
device drivers. 

Then it came to pass that LPARs were invented. Programmers argued the
Second Amendment gave them unrestricted access to memory of other
virtual machines on the same box. Operating system providers were
unmoved; citizens could not own nukes.

Then multi-tasking was invented. At first, Cobol programs had access
to the memory of other tasks because of the Second Amendment.  Bad
things happened. Other tasks got killed because Cobol programs stepped
on them, by accident or deliberately. Cobol programs were killing
themselves. Someone points out we've never needed unrestricted memory
access; we can access variables by name. Other languages, such as C,
once had it and were destroyed by it. Well-mannered programmers get
along fine without it. Let's delete it from the Standard.

Second Amendment advocates reluctantly agree to limiting access to the
task's own memory; machine guns are banned. For good measure, IO port
access is also revoked, since daemons are obsolete; cannons are
banned.  Hardware engineers invent The Safety, which stops writing
over procedural code. That is received as a good thing.

But wait, someone protests, they said Cobol could call daemons. It is
common knowledge that daemons need access to the memory of others AND
IO ports. If Cobol can call daemons, WE can write them. Somebody has
to; they don't write themselves. Never mind that daemons are obsolete,
we should STILL be able to issue IO commands and access anyone's
memory.

Is this a valid argument?
```

```
I may or may not LIKE it, but I don't see anything at ALL ambiguous about

"the right of the people to keep and bear Arms, >shall not be infringed."

"the people" is not limited and what the amendment says is that no government 
(or other institution) shall be allowed to get in the way of the "right to keep 
and bear arms".

It is true that the MOTIVATION (allowing for a "well regulated Militia") 
"limits" the purpose of  the amendment but it does not make what the amendment 
says ambiguous or unclear.
```

```
"Robert Wagner" <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote in message 
news:d25nt09btroebmlnp3s9nu1vv76pnu46ej@4ax.com...
> On Tue, 04 Jan 2005 17:52:29 -0600, LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:
>
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Although a member of the ACLU, I am *not* a constitutional attorney.  I have NOT 
done a search on that specific NY state law.  HOWEVER,

A) many "gun" laws *are* (have been) chanllenged on "constitutional basis".  (If 
that one hasn't been - and I don't know this - it might be in the future).  Some 
have been found to be constitutionally acceptable while others have not.

B) "In general" all of the "bill of rights" amendments have been dealt with at 
various times and places as "trade offs". Certainly, the most famous (as far as 
I know) was the "decision" that "freedom of speech" does NOT allow one to 
(falsely) yell 'fire" in a crowded theatre.

It is possible (but not something that I know for a fact) that some (federal) 
court has determined that the "right" to bear "sub-machne gun nor a rocket 
propelled grenade" in NY State is overriden by some other constitutional right.

  ***

The fact that the amendment is CLEAR that the government shall not "Infringe" on 
such a right does NOT mean that there are not other constitutional rights that 
may (in certain cases) inter-act with this right.

P.S.
   A quick search of the Bill of Rights (first 10 amendments to the 
constitution) indicates that only one other amendment indicates its 
"motivation".

Amendment IV says,

"The right of the people to be secure in their persons, houses, papers, and 
effects, against unreasonable searches and seizures, shall not be violated, and 
no warrants shall issue, but upon probable cause, supported by oath or 
affirmation, and particularly describing the place to be searched, and the 
persons or things to be seized. "

I don't think either the 4th *or* 2nd Amendments provide "motivation" as a 
"restriction" on the amendment.  Both simply provide the (then current thinking) 
that was behind the amendment.

If there were an amendment that said,

"In order to provide adequate roads for stage-coaches, the federal government 
shall provide for adequately paved roads"

this would NOT be invalidated by the "end" of stage-coaches.

Any attempt to "limit" the CURRENT impact of the 2nd amendment based on a 
(perceived) current lack of necessity for "well armed militia" (much less that 
the right to bear arms doesn't seem to - today - provide for a well-armed 
militia) is simply an irrelevant argument.
```

```
I know that realistically there isn't enough time to do this even if
everyone on earth was employed at it).  As far as the 2nd amendment is
concerned - a very good case could be made that there is no longer a
need for a militia AS IT WAS THOUGHT OF then.
```

```
But my point was that REGARDLESS of whether or not there is a current need for a 
"militia" *OR* whether or not guaranteeing the "right to bear arms" actually 
"insures" the existence of a "well armed militia", that the 2nd Amendment makes 
a statement about what the government may or may not do - and that the reference 
to the militia is an explanation of the (original) motivation and has NOTHING to 
do with what "right" the 2nd Amendment does (or does not) provide.

P.S.  Personally, I am in the group that would like to see a new amendment 
"repealing" the 2nd Amendment - but I am also among those who fear "tampering 
with" the Constitution (including its amendments) for anything but the MOST 
important reasons.
```

```
> do with what "right" the 2nd Amendment does (or does not) provide.

I can appreciate what you say, but I feel that since the framers of the
amendment felt it necessary to provide a rationale that they didn't see
it as applying at all times and under all circumstances.  It's a sort of
sunset clause with unspecified duration.  You're quite right that as
long as the 2nd amendment remains in the constitution then it
(supposedly) constrains the government.
> 
> P.S.  Personally, I am in the group that would like to see a new amendment
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> --
Agreed on both points.

Did you ever read Robert Heinlein's "The moon is a harsh mistress"?  At
one point his hero proposes a branch of government whose sole purpose is
to cancel laws!  Might be something in the idea!

PL
```

```
On Thu, 6 Jan 2005 14:35:06 GMT, "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>
wrote:

>And I agree with that point.  And I reiterate:
>The 2nd amendment is on the books.    It should be enforced or repealed.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>I'd be quite satisfied it it were repealed.   But don't wiggle around it.

Three-fourths of the States will never vote for repeal.

When lawyers smell blood in the water, they'll be all over the gun
industry like sharks on a sinking whale. In the news:

"Washington, DC (10/20/04) - Recently, the Indiana Supreme Court, Ohio
Supreme Court, and appellate courts in New Jersey and Illinois have
reinstated cases brought by cities in those states. In the last few
months, courts have also approved a record $4.4 million of settlements
against gun sellers and manufacturers in cases brought by gun violence
victims. This includes an unprecedented $2.5 million settlement in a
suit brought by victims of the DC-area sniper attacks against gun shop
Bull's Eye Shooter Supply of Tacoma, Washington, and gun maker
Bushmaster Firearms of Windham, Maine. Those victims also brought suit
alleging that the gun shop and gun maker acted negligently and created
a public nuisance. Gun shop Bull's Eye claimed that it "lost" 238 guns
in a three year period, including the DC sniper's Bushmaster assault
weapon.

Pennsylvania (10/20/04) - The ruling in Allegheny County Sportsmen's
League v. Rendell holds that Pennsylvania law allows police to keep a
database of handgun sales records. The gun lobby had argued that such
records must be destroyed, citing a state law prohibiting the
maintenance of gun "ownership" records. The Court rejected this
challenge, declaring that "maintenance of a database of handgun sales
is proper" under Pennsylvania law, as such a database tracks only
handgun sales records, not ownership. The court relied in part on a
friend of the court amicus brief filed by law enforcement and gun
violence prevention groups, including the Brady Center to Prevent Gun
Violence, the Pennsylvania Million Mom March and the Pennsylvania
Chiefs of Police Association.

Philadelphia, PA (8/20/04) - A Pennsylvania gun dealer has settled a
pioneering lawsuit brought by a woman whose seven-year-old son was
unintentionally killed near his Philadelphia home with a gun sold by
the dealer. The settlement will have nationwide implications for gun
dealers who supply traffickers with guns - transactions which occur
everyday in gun shops around the country.

The suit, brought by Tennille Jefferson against Williamsport gun
dealer Sauers Trading, charged Sauers with supplying ten guns to gun
trafficker Perry Bruce. Although Sauers sold the guns to Bruce
legally, Bruce purchased the handguns repeatedly, with thousands of
dollars in cash, even while telling Sauers he was unemployed and on
welfare. The guns were trafficked to Philadelphia, where one was found
under a parked car by a group of children and used to unintentionally
shoot and kill Nafis Jefferson on April 19, 1999. "

http://www.gunlawsuits.org/
```

```
public policy.
```

```
> On  5-Jan-2005, Joe Zitzelberger <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote:

Here are the numbers you asked for.  It took some doing, I actually used 
the M$ Excel product -- I really want to exercise some 2nd amendment 
rights upon whomever designed that stupid little Mac128 agent.  (the 
paperclip in the windoze version)

I took the DOD population representation survey and mixed it with the 
2004 electoral vote.  It shows military enlistment by percent of 
population by state and enlistments per electoral vote.  

You can find the full table at "www.zitzelberger.net/index.html".  

The data used was for 2002 enlistments, the most recent year available. 
D.C. is included as a state because I had data for it.  The population 
figures are from the reapportionment census.

Some interesting details:

   - Washington, The first blue state to appear is 19th ranked in 
military enlistment.

   - Only 3 blue states appear in the top 30.

   - The bottom of the table is solid blue, the top, solid red.  This 
tends to support my contention that the army is populated by 'red state 
types'.

Enjoy.
```

```
On Tue, 11 Jan 2005 09:26:30 -0500, Joe Zitzelberger
<joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote:

>> > I think you fail to understand that the Army is overwhelmingly populated
>> > by red-state types that tend to not trust the government in the first
>> > place.  The blue-state pussies rarely join up.

I think they join because of their state's economy, not because of
ideology. After leaving high school, an 18-19 year-old has three
choices:

1.  Get a job
  The states near the top of the list are primarily rural, offering
relatively fewer job opportunities. When's the last time you saw an ad
for a programming job in Wyoming or Montana?
  The states near the bottom of the list are urbanized and have better
job opportunities.

2.  Go to college
  In rural places, fewer parents can afford to send their kid to
college.
  In blue states, kids with the intelligence (and some without) are
pushed into college by parents and peers. That's why the Ivy League is
in the NorthEast rather than Texas.

3.  Join the military
  Requires no money nor high intelligence. Offers some their only
chance to go to college. Promises more adventure than flipping
burgers.
```

```
always required, some college is preferred.  Dropouts, misfits and 
criminals haven't been the standard troop for decades.

NCOs these days are almost required to get an Associates degree in 
management or an MOS related field.  A BS is a de facto requirement 
assuming they want to grow up to be senior NCOs.
```

```
would be getting.
```

```
On 12 Jan 2005 18:23:54 -0800, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>>> Or are you expecting that a 17 year old can find a 'real job' ?
>
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
>and some will be worse off for having gone.

College admission and funding is a selection process. It may be that
its selection of 'alphas' vs. others accounts for the difference in
earnings. To test that hypothesis, a study would compare non-college
high school graduates with an IQ over 120 vs. non-college high school
graduates 120 and under, or use another measure such as SAT score
(college entrance exam taken by most high schoolers). I've not seen
such a study.

This summary comes close
http://www.window.state.tx.us/specialrpt/highered/earnings.html

"Lost earnings while attending college, average just over $20,000 per
year for high school graduates and are, by far, the most significant
cost in pursuing a higher education degree."

In table 3.3, it compares high school grads to those with "some
college". In other words, gone through the selection process but lack
a complete college education. It finds return on investment from
getting in and dropping out -- 15% -- is higher than staying four
years -- 11%. However, the net present value of the earnings gain,
less direct and indirect expensesï¿½including foregone incomeï¿½averages
$108,700 for batchelor's degree vs. 37,624 for "some college". A
finding that surprised me is associate (two year) degree is almost as
high as batchelor's in money return and the same as "some college" in
percentage return -- $74,299 and 15%.  

Masters and doctoral both returned 12%. Professional (doctors,
lawyers) returned 14%. 

It could be argued that college makes one a 'good corporate citizen'
by teaching him to follow instructions, work hard, socialize and play
politics. Perhaps those skills can be learned in two years, and a
portion of college dropouts don't have those skills.
```

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>

```

"Robert Wagner" <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:gaeau09o7d7lcmaugnuunndg4h82u9v1ni@4ax.com...

> Move. Florida has inexpensive housing, jobs and good weather.

Yeah.  The weather's been *just fantastic* over the past year in particular!


> The greatest cost of college is four years of lost wages -- roughly
> $150-200,000.

$37,500/yr as a starting salary -- much less $50,000/yr -- straight out of
high school would be an expectation that would most likely generate
horselaughs out here in SoCal.  Maybe that's  not the case elsewhere in the
country, but in all honesty I can't fathom where.


> I predict lower standards when they realize we need an additional
> million bodies. The Army can't even put 150,000 in Iraq without
> calling up reservists. What will happen when Korea blows up, or we
> have to defend Taiwan? If we don't have the manpower, the only option
> will be a draft.

And reinstating the draft automatically and unconditionally results in lower
standards ... exactly how?   With the demise of the cold war "critical
defense industry" status for an employee is far less likely than once it
was, and the practice of the "educational deferment" isn't exactly a
Constitutional right!

I suspect what will happen when Korea blows up is that Korea will blow up,
and I suspect what will happen when Taiwan gets attacked is that Taiwan gets
attacked.

Treaties and history to the contrary notwithstanding, neither is
*intrinsically* the US' direct business, any more than the internal politics
of Cuba or the Philippines today, both of which *arguably* should have been
treated as Spoils of War after 1898 and made territories of the United
States!

As has been evidenced in Europe, a lot of countries are getting tired of the
US attitude that it's their God-given right to rap the knuckles of any
country whose behavior doesn't fit what the US thinks is right.  In the
cited cases, we may intervene and we may not, and either way the long-term
consequences of our intervention in and of itself are at least as serious as
the external acts that led to them!

There's a lot about the US that reminds me of England as the members of the
Commonwealth moved off into self-rule.  Empires rise and empires fall.  "The
sun never sets on the British Empire" is no longer true, and much as we
would like to believe in the Monroe Doctrine, Manifest Destiny, and the
invincibility of the Great White Fleet, those days are, alas, a century and
more past.

The US is still a world power, but it can no longer afford to be the power
it once was and to wield the might it once wielded, and I think we'd be
perceived rather better as a nation if we accepted that a little more
gracefully and maturely instead of continuing to presume our right is to be
final arbiter and ultimate disciplinarian to the rest of the world.   The
more we continue to act that way, the more likely it is that we'll get our
butt kicked.

        -Chuck Stevens
```

- **References:** `<dBSCd.228686$2W1.22704@news.easynews.com> <41DC2CFB.228C27BD@mb.sympatico.ca> <joe_zitzelberger-7827E2.21525905012005@knology.usenetserver.com> <crjiis$ju4$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <joe_zitzelberger-3A7D6D.09262811012005@knology.usenetserver.com> <9v68u01vt9o31es2htr1pglutf4981rl4q@4ax.com> <joe_zitzelberger-05174E.18373111012005@knology.usenetserver.com> <gaeau09o7d7lcmaugnuunndg4h82u9v1ni@4ax.com> <cs3mmu$sch$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <qjpau01g372bakj9s237hrber8jepinb09@4ax.com>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:qjpau01g372bakj9s237hrber8jepinb09@4ax.com...

> >  With the demise of the cold war "critical
> >defense industry" status for an employee is far less likely than once it
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Hello Canada.

Depends on how much you agree with the policies of the country, now, doesn't
it!


> >I suspect what will happen when Korea blows up is that Korea will blow
up,
> >and I suspect what will happen when Taiwan gets attacked is that Taiwan
gets
> >attacked.
>
> Failing to defend South Korea would seriously hurt American prestige
> with our friends.

Who's our friends?   A good many of our friends aren't happy with our
"defending our interests" in the middle east by overthrowing Hussein!  I saw
the *large* display of posters attacking US policy on a lawn next to
Westminster Abbey on Remembrance Day!   The Brits aren't too happy with
Blair for supporting us, and a good deal of Europe is upset with us over our
*interventionism*.


> That's why we backed down in Somalia and Lebanon, didn't go into
> Rwanda, didn't help South Africa when it was attacked by Cuba.

As I understand it, we no longer recognize Taiwan as an independent country
but rather sovereign territory of Mainland China.  By so doing we've
publically acknowledged that attacking Taiwan is tantamount to attacking
Beijing, so it seems to me we've put ourselves in a box that leaves Taiwan
pretty much hanging out to dry!   Now we should come to their defense and
*invade China* if China moves troops to its own territory?

And if, say, Japan or the Philippines, New Zealand or Australia -- or even
Italy or Turkey -- find the idea of a Unified Korea so threatening, why
don't they match us, soldier-for-soldier, in resolving the threat?

> That's why we shouldn't waste our limited resources in places like
> Iraq. We should hold them in reserve for real emergencies.

Well, I'm a cynic in this respect.  We were preparing for war for some time
before 1941, and we went to war in 1941, and we waged the Cold War for
decades more, and the deficit spending justified by defense needs pulled us
out of the Depression and made our economy boom.   The end of the Cold War
brought the end of the massive defense expenditures, and Lower Taxes and
Reduced Deficit became the cry.   We do not always go to war out of
Righteous Indignation or even Wounded Pride; I think we go to war sometimes
to protect our *economic* interests.

Panama was part of Colombia until 1903.  We stationed troops in Panama and
*then* Panama declared its independence from Colombia.  I do not believe
sympathy for the Poor Downtrodden Panamanian Victims of the Vile Oppressor
Colombians was very much a part of the equation; I suspect rather more
strongly, in fact, that the Panamanian Independence Movement amounted to an
sparse extremist group until we decided to jump in and finish what the
French had started.   There's not a whole lot to support a booming
Panamanian economy outside that which is brought to it by the Canal.  We
stationed troops there because we wanted control over the Canal, and we were
willing to wrest an asset from Colombia that Colombia (and arguably the rest
of South America) would *really* rather we hadn't wrested from them in order
to accomplish that.   But it was our Bounden Duty, don'tcha know.

> This morning I talked to a friend who's an expert in the Middle East.
> I asked whether he could name a single Arab country where democracy
> has worked. He said "There's one: Palestine." I said that's not a
> country.  He said "Sure it is, it's an occupied country."

No doubt.  My brother-in-law, a native of Venezuela raised on Trinidad and
educated in England and the US,  has stated repeatedly that there are many
countries in which the most effective and most productive approach to
government is the benevolent dictatorship.   Just as unionization doesn't
*always* end up being in the best interest of the worker, so democracy (in
whichever form) isn't *always* in the best interest of the citizen.

I've seen a democracy in operation that is, I believe, purer than we have
here (France), and although after seeing Versailles I understand why, I
don't think Vox Populi, Vox Dei is always, or even usually, the case, and I
wasn't all that comfortable with what I saw of the way things happen there.
DeTocqueville warned of the tyranny of the majority in the US; where I see
it exemplified much more strongly is in France.  What I saw there (and I
can't think of specific examples) struck me as even more capricious than
even the worst-conceived of the California "There oughta be a LAW!!!"
propositions.   Unfettered democracy ain't what it's cracked up to be.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
"Government" is a collective noun which subsumes all the people who work
in or for the governing body of the country.  As such, the elected
portion tends to reflect the characteristics of the electorate - which
as a whole is not well educated, well read, or even very attentive to
the issues.  (Not just in the US, everywhere with free elections).  And
("Yes Minister" notwithstanding) since the elected control the
government that means that it is a poorly educated, poorly read,
inattentive organism.  You gets what you pays for!

So it's no wonder that you had to do guard duty with unloaded weapons. 
I'd wonder if somewhere in your chain of command there wasn't somebody
on the take.

Still and all, I'll keep to my opinion.  Ordinary citizens can't defend
themselves against the government unless they can muster similar
resources.  

PL
```

- **From:** Joe Zitzelberger <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com>
- **Date:** 2005-01-11T09:44:00-05:00

```
I've often heard the 'I only signed up for two/three/four years' 
comments.  That limited time was never guaranteed, it was always 8 more 
years every time you signed.  I tend to think that if any redefining is 
being done, it is by the person trying to avoid the remainder of their 8.

I remember some people being shocked in 1990 that the IMA contract they 
signed upon leaving the service was also for 8 years.  They thought they 
just got to train for a few weeks and get a check...then they got called 
up.


> > That applies to enlistment and reenlistment contracts -- officers are
> > for life unless the resign their commission.
> 
> I have been honorably discharged twice - once from active duty, and once from
> the reserves.   Since I was an officer did that mean anything?

I will admit, I'm not the most knowledgeable about officer personnel 
management -- and all of my limited knowledge is restricted to the Army.

Aren't you subject to lifetime callback unless you resign your 
commission?  Or within reason -- I know the Army gives up on medical 
officers at 68 or 72, other branches somewhat younger.
```

```

"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message
news:joe_zitzelberger-E7F3C8.22250510012005@knology.usenetserver.com...

> That applies to enlistment and reenlistment contracts -- officers are
> for life unless the resign their commission.

In the Navy back in the 1960's, as I recall the rule was if you were passed
over three times for promotion you were out of the service; if you were
eligible for promotion and weren't an absolute disaster as an officer and
were lucky you might get a "pre-retirement" pro-forma promotion; if you were
eligible for retirement (20 years?) you could retire; if you weren't yet
eligible for retirement, you were pretty much SOL no matter how much more
time you expected to be in the service.  Has that changed?

    -Chuck Stevens

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```

"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message
news:joe_zitzelberger-0F8801.18543611012005@knology.usenetserver.com...

> I don't think that has changed.
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> the 1970s old -- way past any recent contract signing old.

That could well be, and the higher the rank the more likely that is.   I
gather that medical personnel are handled somewhat differently from line
officers; my brother-in-law was Army reserve enlisted, served in the tank
corps in Desert Storm, came back, got his nursing degree, and got
commissioned (IIRC) as a Captain in the Army right off the bat.

I can't think of any case in which a Navy line officer -- as distinct from
Medical, Dental, Nurse, Medical Service, Chaplian (Christian, Jewish and
Muslim), Supply, Civil Engineer and JAG Corps -- Chaplain, Civil
Engineering, Supply, etc. Corps -- might get such a leg up right out of
school.

The earlier a USN or USNR line officer gets passed over for promotion, the
less likely he is to be pulled back in, I'd guess.  If a career line officer
gets passed over three times for Lieutenant Commander, that's a really
strong indication that the officers reviewing your performance were Not At
All Pleased, and that virtually nobody was going to bat for you.   On the
other hand, it strikes me that being blindsided by getting passed over three
times for LCDR would illustrate exactly the sort of cluelessness that would
have led to being passed over three times for LCDR in the first place!

(As for me, I didn't make it as far as Midshipman Third Class, USN ...
;-(         )

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
In article <joe_zitzelberger-0F8801.18543611012005@knology.usenetserver.com>,
Joe Zitzelberger  <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote:

[snip]

>Just recently the Army called up a bunch of old doctors and 
>psychiatrists -- I mean 65+ and 70+ old -- retired from active duty in 
>the 1970s old -- way past any recent contract signing old.

Heh... reminds me of a fellow I knew, enlisted in the Signal Corps after 
Pearl Harbor, got into the Army Air Corps... blew out his back, L4/L5 on 
the obstacle course, spent 54 weeks in the hospital, the first ten in full 
body traction.  He was discharged as 100% disabled, worked his way out of 
his leg braces while he finished college on the GI bill...

... but the quotas of the time didn't allow for too many of His Kind into 
medical school so he went overseas, came back to the States and did his 
residency in psychiatry... settled down into a nice practise in the early 
'60s...

... and then got a letter from the Army... they remembered him and kept 
track of him and said that even though he mustered out as a corporal 
they'd give him a full bird (colonel) if he'd come back... and promised, 
in writing, a star (brigadier general) if he re-upped after six years.

He told me about the offer and I said 'Not a bad deal, really... and think 
of the programs you'd be able to set up for them.  What're you gonna tell 
'em?'

His said 'I already responded; I told them 'Please take my name off your 
mailing list.''

DD
```

```
Perhaps the rules have changed.  I grew up in an area into which a WHOLE
BUNCH of Air Force (and some Army) officers retired, namely, the Hill
Country of Texas.  San Antonio had Lackland AFB (basic training), Kelly AFB
(major maintenance base), Brooks Field (lots of aerospace and medical
research) and Randolph AFB ("The West Point of the Air" until the USAFA was
built), along with Fort Sam Houston for the Army, and these were popular
places to retire from a few decades back.  Many of those I knew were
Lieutenant Colonels that were granted their promotion only immediately
before retirement.  The ones who spent any time at all as "light colonels"
generally retired as "full bird colonels".   The few retired one-stars of my
acquaintance left no doubt in their demeanor that they didn't have much
practice or expertise in the sort of politics and social graces that is, I
believe, required even at entry into that level and I believe they all
received their stars along with their bye-bye papers.  "Light colonels"
insisted on being referred to and addressed as "colonel" in casual
conversation, and my distinct impression, in general, was that they were
Majors that got left there because they had reached the level of their
incompetence.   "Retired Air Force Lieutenant Colonel" was, in fact, rather
a stereotype in my youth, a stereotype that generally triggered a snicker
from the general populace! .

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
On Thu, 06 Jan 2005 17:29:45 -0600, LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:

>Peter Lacey wrote:
>> 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>http://www.cato.org/dailys/05-13-00.html

"4. States that allow registered citizens to carry concealed weapons
have lower crime rates than those that don't. 

True. The 31 states that have "shall issue" laws allowing private
citizens to carry concealed weapons have, on average, a 24 percent
lower violent crime rate, a 19 percent lower murder rate and a 39
percent lower robbery rate than states that forbid concealed weapons.
In fact, the nine states with the lowest violent crime rates are all
right-to-carry states. Remarkably, guns are used for self-defense more
than 2 million times a year, three to five times the estimated number
of violent crimes committed with guns."

That's NOT true. Let's look at New York, which has the strictest gun
laws, compared to Texas, which has the loosest.

"In the year 2000 New York had an estimated population of 18,976,457
which ranked the state 3rd in population. For that year the State of
New York had a total Crime Index of 3,099.6 reported incidents per
100,000 people. This ranked the state as having the 40th highest total
Crime Index. For Violent Crime New York had a reported incident rate
of 553.9 per 100,000 people. This ranked the state as having the 12th
highest occurrence for Violent Crime among the states. For crimes
against Property, the state had a reported incident rate of 2,545.7
per 100,000 people, which ranked as the state 45th highest. Also in
the year 2000 New York had 5.0 Murders per 100,000 people, ranking the
state as having the 23rd highest rate for Murder.

In the year 2000 Texas had an estimated population of 20,851,820 which
ranked the state 2nd in population. For that year the State of Texas
had a total Crime Index of 4,955.5 reported incidents per 100,000
people. This ranked the state as having the 8th highest total Crime
Index. For Violent Crime Texas had a reported incident rate of 545.1
per 100,000 people. This ranked the state as having the 13th highest
occurrence for Violent Crime among the states. For crimes against
Property, the state had a reported incident rate of 4,410.4 per
100,000 people, which ranked as the state 10th highest. Also in the
year 2000 Texas had 5.9 Murders per 100,000 people, ranking the state
as having the 17th highest rate for Murder."

Because you're in Alabama, which also has liberal gun laws:

"In the year 2000 Alabama had an estimated population of 4,447,100
which ranked the state 23rd in population. For that year the State of
Alabama had a total Crime Index of 4,545.9 reported incidents per
100,000 people. This ranked the state as having the 15th highest total
Crime Index. For Violent Crime Alabama had a reported incident rate of
486.2 per 100,000 people. This ranked the state as having the 20th
highest occurrence for Violent Crime among the states. For crimes
against Property, the state had a reported incident rate of 4,059.7
per 100,000 people, which ranked as the state 15th highest. Also in
the year 2000 Alabama had 7.4 Murders per 100,000 people, ranking the
state as having the 6th highest rate for Murder."
http://www.disastercenter.com/crime/alcrime.htm

The idea that gun ownership is a deterrent to crime is a fiction.
Statistics have never shown that to be true.They show that Bubba,
pissed becase his wife is screwing the neighbor, participates in a
crime of passion that wouldn't have occurred sans firearms.

I've shown that more than 50% of firearm deaths result from crimes of
passion rather than premeditated or criminal activities.  When you add
accidents and suicides, the total goes to more than 80%.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: "Right to bear arms"

_(reply depth: 31)_

- **From:** Robert Wagner <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net>
- **Date:** 2005-01-07T16:43:07+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<obctt0htiue8i5ifn0jjie33hectfdl6ek@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<33n6eeF4229heU1@individual.net> <41DC2CFB.228C27BD@mb.sympatico.ca> <PRjDd.1304$fR3.727@fe40.usenetserver.com> <5ctrt0hp92ks9v0oe0p8bu3he2eqbqsqpt@4ax.com> <crlnr8$eeo$1@panix5.panix.com>`

```
On 7 Jan 2005 05:21:28 -0500, docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

>In article <5ctrt0hp92ks9v0oe0p8bu3he2eqbqsqpt@4ax.com>,
>Robert Wagner  <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote:
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
>be able to tell you.

As everyone familiar with statistics knows, correlation does not show
causation. There is often a third variable, called a confounder, that
causes both variables under investigation. 

In this case there are two confounders: population density and age. It
is well known that crime goes up as population density increases. It
is also well known that most crimes are committed by young people.
Declining crime rate over the last ten years is explained by aging of
the demographic bubble called baby boomers.

Generally, the 31 states with 'shall issue' laws are sparsely
populated ones on the West. I don't know whether their average age is
higher or lower.

Density is not as simple as (population / area). Arizona has the
highest crime rate and seemingly low density. But 40% of its
population live in two high-density cities. Texas, too, has a large
area but 90% of its population lives in the eastern third, mostly in
big cities.

An honest correlation between gun law and crime would do a regression
analysis on density and age, then discount their effect from each
state's crime rate. Statisticians at the Cato Institute surely know
that; they conveniently 'forgot' to do it right.
```

- **From:** Robert Wagner <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a5ott09ovsh98isp34rbm3dqfa47ltemg8@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<33n6eeF4229heU1@individual.net> <5ctrt0hp92ks9v0oe0p8bu3he2eqbqsqpt@4ax.com> <crlnr8$eeo$1@panix5.panix.com> <obctt0htiue8i5ifn0jjie33hectfdl6ek@4ax.com> <crmf6h$bpj$1@panix5.panix.com>`

```
On 7 Jan 2005 12:00:01 -0500, docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

>In article <obctt0htiue8i5ifn0jjie33hectfdl6ek@4ax.com>,
>Robert Wagner  <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote:


>>An honest correlation between gun law and crime would do a regression
>>analysis on density and age, then discount their effect from each
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>Wagner, and yet you have not demonstrated this honesty in your analysis.

It's too much work for an off-topic discussion. I did an analysis 15
years ago that found guns do more damage than they prevent. The
fallacy in pro-gun arguments is assuming everyone who shoots another
in a crime of passion is 'a criminal'. It's a circular argument. Of
course they're criminals a postiori (unless they can afford Johnny
Cochran). I argued they wouldn't have become criminals if there hadn't
been guns at hand.

Note that the Cato Institute says crimes *of violence* are lower in
states with liberal gun laws. Why exclude property crime? The
deterrent effect of a gun in the house is principally against
burglary. I'll tell you why. Because in sparsely populated states, the
ones that have liberal gun laws, most crime is against property; in
densely populated states, it's proportionally more against people. If
guns didn't exist, the violent crime rate in rural states would still
be lower than urban states. 

There is no way of directly measuring crimes prevented by guns. By
definition, they're unreported. The best we can do is imply them by
comparing crimes that were reported against an otherwise identical
baseline place with no guns. How about another country? There are many
to choose from, because very few countries have as many guns as the
US. In 2000, the homicide rate for all E.U. countries was 1.7 per
100,000; for the US it was 5.9.
```

```
This isn't as irrelevant as it sounds:  My brother-in-law grew up on
Trinidad where, I'm reasonably certain, private ownership of guns was
prohibited.  He hung out in some pretty seedy dives in and around
Port-of-Spain in his youth, and he comment that "cutlass" (machete) fights
were about as common there as fist-fights were twenty years ago here in the
States.   And just about everybody in the laboring classes had machetes;
they were a handy tool in both the rain forests and in agriculture.

Point being:  Where there's a will, there's a way (and a weapon).   *Guns*
don't kill people; it's *machetes* that kill people!     ;-)
```

```
low-probability, long-term effects such as subjugation to a tyrannical

from cars and guns is the same:  30,000 Americans per year.  The
benefit of cars is obvious; the benefit of letting Bubba own a deer
rifle, and 30 other Bubbas owning illegal unregistered guns, is not so
obvious. Especially when they use the weapons against each other more
than against deer.
```

```
In article <455ut0hm1mcvglaipc2gk9jvjarf9o4a3u@4ax.com>,
 Robert Wagner <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote:

> On Fri, 7 Jan 2005 20:47:53 GMT, "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>
> wrote:
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
> than against deer.

The Bureau of Justice Statistics list firearms as responsible for 2/3rds 
of 2003 homicides here 
"http://www.ojp.usdoj.gov/bjs/glance/guncrime.htm".

It also lists a homicide rate of 6.1 per 100,000 for 2000 (I excluded 
the 2001 numbers because the 9/11 bombings skewed them).  You can find 
that number here "http://www.ojp.usdoj.gov/bjs/glance/hmrt.htm".

The population of the US was 281 million in 2000 -- which leads us to a 
figure of 17,141 homicides in the US for 2000 (281M/100K * 6.1).

Again, using the official crime statistics, we find that 11,484 of those 
homicides were committed with a firearm (17,141*.67).

For your number of 30k/year to be accurate, you would need 18,516 
accidental deaths from firearms.  You can find the number of accidental 
firearms deaths here -- claiming census bureau figures from 1999 -- 
"http://www.anesi.com/accdeath.htm".  The put the accidental deaths at 
1,134, with no idea how many of those might have been suicides.

You your 30k number is left with a deficit of 17,382 firearms deaths.  
You have managed to overstate by almost three times the number of annual 
firearms deaths -- is it possible you have some sort of agenda rather 
than an interest in the facts?


[
You could argue (and you probably will), that firearms deaths that are 
ruled suicides make up your 17k shortfall.  However, to argue such would 
be as disingenuous as lumping 19-year-old gang members shooting each 
other in with "children" to secure a properly horrific figure.  

We know from long study that access to a firearm might influence the 
style of suicide -- but removing the firearm from a suicidal person only 
shifts them to another tool.  In short, guns don't cause suicide, 
dysfunctional people who cannot or will not cope with their problems do.  
Unless you want Rope-control, Car-control, Knife-control, Pill-control, 
etc...
]
```

```
Germany, in the late 19th century, had a larger number of nesting-sites
```

- **Message-ID:** `<1104807021.788374.183580@c13g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`

- **References:** `<31usn2F39796fU1@individual.net> <cpkdi4$lds$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <59pvd.7413$ZP5.7342@fe40.usenetserver.com> <cpn2m3$ak4$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <V%KAd.3681$Kw1.1871@fe40.usenetserver.com> <vtn7t0h3bnqaaljti633a72o6fq93rr36t@4ax.com> <zR_Ad.7505$Kw1.2025@fe40.usenetserver.com> <7dk9t0diouj568spjbchdl61v28iqim3a8@4ax.com> <33n6eeF4229heU1@individual.net> <jkjdt0lv5msqckvtn2bjoamujjb4n9n2qm@4ax.com> <1104778898.477277.310300@c13g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <q1mjt0h91jltnub43hkk5ntqnuas8uj3g4@4ax.com>`

```
> Blank rounds contain more powder, up to three times as much,

So, you now claim that a blank cartridge for a rifle would be 3 times
the size of a live cartridge (less the bullet).  What nonsense, chamber
sizes are fixed for a particular gun.

Having fired .303 blanks and .303 live the sound is about the same
level and the powder load was insignificantly different (though it
would be much faster burning).

In fact starter pistol rounds are smaller than .22 short rounds.

It is true that black powder cannons must cut down the charge when
firing a ball, but that is a barrel pressure issue.

> So about 1/3 of the bang comes from exploding powder and 2/3 from the
projectile.

You just made that up, and probably by extrapolating from the
misinformation about '3 times' above.  The proportion for each part is
entirely dependent on individual factors and will vary enormously.  No
general figure can be given any credence at all.

In fact if it were true in any way then 'silencers' would hardly work
at all.  If 2/3 were from the projectile then that 2/3 would not be
affected by a silencer.  A silencer works by baffling the gas expansion
after the bullet has left the barrel and restricting it so that it is
over a longer time period.  It is effective because it is the explosion
that makes the noise and spreading the pressure rise over a longer time
reduces the peak of the pressure wave.

> The sound from the gun is directed; the projectile's sonic boom is
> omnidirectional.

So your claim is that you cannot hear a gun if standing behind or
alongside it.  If you knew anything about 'sonic booms' you would not
make a silly claim that it was 'omnidirectional'.

The major part of the noise is made by the expanding gasses at the
point when the bullet leaves the barrel and the residual pressure can
freely expand.  With the same cartridge a longer barrel is quieter even
though the muzzle velocity may be greater because there is less
residual expansion to occur and the pressure will be correspondingly
lower by having had a larger volume to expand in prior to becoming
open.

> Also, the boom-cone is about ten feet above your ears, so its
footprint is smaller.

Just make it up as you go along, you have no idea how far it was from
my ears.

In any case, by your theory standing 10 foot from a gun, alongside it,
should not be much louder than if one was, say, a couple hundred metres
down range 10 foot from the path.

Based on my experience, this is complete crap.

> Smaller quantity and slower burning (black powder).
Exactly. Small amount of slower burning powder = much quieter.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT Science versus religion: Is compromise impossible?

_(reply depth: 24)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-01-04T10:53:05-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1104864785.468067.218700@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<7ikkt0hrjisj6q00g779tei067svvb2149@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<cpn2m3$ak4$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <V%KAd.3681$Kw1.1871@fe40.usenetserver.com> <vtn7t0h3bnqaaljti633a72o6fq93rr36t@4ax.com> <zR_Ad.7505$Kw1.2025@fe40.usenetserver.com> <7dk9t0diouj568spjbchdl61v28iqim3a8@4ax.com> <33n6eeF4229heU1@individual.net> <jkjdt0lv5msqckvtn2bjoamujjb4n9n2qm@4ax.com> <1104778898.477277.310300@c13g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <q1mjt0h91jltnub43hkk5ntqnuas8uj3g4@4ax.com> <1104807021.788374.183580@c13g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <7ikkt0hrjisj6q00g779tei067svvb2149@4ax.com>`

```
> Shell casings are not filled with powder. There's plenty of room for
> additional powder.

Geez, Robert, when cornered, you just increase the rate that you make
nonsense up.

Cartridges do not rattle when shaken.  They are not '1/3 full'. They
make cartridges of exactly the size required to hold the powder and the
bullet tightly. They do not 'leave space so that they can add more
powder for blanks' (or any other reason).

Shotgun cartridges are quite different from this because they also have
to hold the shot and the wadding or sabot.  But rifle and handgun
cartridges are always full.

>> In fact starter pistol rounds are smaller than .22 short rounds.

> Again, there's plenty of space for additional powder.

The whole point of being a .22 'short' is that it has less powder than
a .22 'long'.  The cartridge size is reduced so that there _isn't_
'plenty of space'.  There is never empty space left in a cartridge.

>> If you knew anything about 'sonic booms' you would not
>> make a silly claim that it was 'omnidirectional'.

> Ok, it's directed to the front, but not toward the ground.

You seem to know even less about sonic booms than even I would credit
you with.

Have you never been under a supersonic aircraft at 500 feet ? (I have).
They are completely and utterly silent 'to the front', they are silent
even after they have passed overhead until they are at about 45 degrees
beyond.  After the bang the sound recedes quickly.

The amount of noise in the bang depends on many different factors, such
as overpressure generated by the lift from the wings (which is thus
proprtional to weight), interference from the many parts of the
structure, and is also an accumulation of the noise from the engine.
That is, all the time the plane was completely silent as it approched
the noise it was making (engine etc) just hadn't reached you yet.  It
reaches you all at once.

None of this applies to bullets (but does to rockets) because they
don't, of themselves, make noise.  They have no wings to create
overpressure from lift, they are only a single shape so have no
interference.  Bullets do create a 'sonic boom' but it is trivial
compared to the unsilenced explosion that drives it.

> Silencers .. They can deaden the
> explosion but can't do much about the zing of of supersonic flight.

Exactly.  They deaden much of the explosion.  The 'zing' is just a
small part of the noise.
Your 'few people know' is just more misinformation.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT Science versus religion: Is compromise impossible?

_(reply depth: 25)_

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>

```
On  4-Jan-2005, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

> None of this applies to bullets (but does to rockets) because they
> don't, of themselves, make noise.  They have no wings to create
> overpressure from lift, they are only a single shape so have no
> interference.  Bullets do create a 'sonic boom' but it is trivial
> compared to the unsilenced explosion that drives it.

The bullets do make noise.   The sonic boom we hear is similar to the crack of a
whip (for the same reason).   It is not similar to the boom of the firing.

Shotguns are loud, even though they do not create shots that break the sound
barrier.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT Science versus religion: Is compromise impossible?

_(reply depth: 26)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-01-04T13:36:56-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1104874616.767756.4040@c13g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<creqf6$g2t$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<cpn2m3$ak4$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <1104864785.468067.218700@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <creqf6$g2t$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
>>  they don't, of themselves, make noise.

> The bullets do make noise.

They don't, of themselves, make noise.  Unlike a Jet Aircraft, bullets
do not generate noise while stationary (and running).

>> Bullets do create a 'sonic boom'

> The sonic boom we hear is similar to the crack of a whip (for the
same reason).

Mostly.  A supersonic plane's sonic boom is not just the 'bow wave', it
is also the accumulation of noise from its engines, etc.  When a
subsonic jet flies towards you you can hear it for all the time it
approaches, the sound energy is spread over a long time.  When flying
at Mach 1 towards you all that sound energy arrives at the same time,
along with the 'bow wave' of pressure.

When it flies faster, such as a Concorde at Mach 2.2, it gets
'downrange' faster than the sound can get to ground level, so it gets
60 deg from vertically above (ie is only 30 deg above the horizon)
before you can hear _anything_.  Then there is a diffuse boom (from ten
miles above) followed by a rumble for some minutes from points along
its track as the noise arrives, initially from directly above, then
moving along the track both ways.

Those 4 Olympus in after burning are _loud_.  I haven't been under a
Concorde at full speed but have been at the end of a runway as one took
off (with afterburners) over me.

Whether the 'crack of the whip' is _entirely_ due to the tip going
sonic is another argument.  For example I get exactly the same noise
from a strap by folding it over and, holding it in two hands,
'snapping' it.  The strap never gets close to moving at supersonic
speed, but the air ejected laterally probably does.
```

```
On 4 Jan 2005 10:53:05 -0800, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>> Shell casings are not filled with powder. There's plenty of room for
>> additional powder.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>powder for blanks' (or any other reason).

Go to this Web site:
http://www.loadyourown.com/loaddata/sdata.asp

Select Rifle - .45 Colt and look at the range of loads. They go from 7
to 30 grains. Then try Pistol - .357 Magnum, which ranges from 3 to
20. Unexpectedly, muzzle velocity is not linear to the load.

>Shotgun cartridges are quite different from this because they also have
>to hold the shot and the wadding or sabot.  But rifle and handgun
>cartridges are always full.

No, they're not.

>>> In fact starter pistol rounds are smaller than .22 short rounds.
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>'plenty of space'.  There is never empty space left in a cartridge.

I can't find data on .22 blank loads, so the question is undecided.
It's safe to say explosion rate is faster.

>>> If you knew anything about 'sonic booms' you would not
>>> make a silly claim that it was 'omnidirectional'.
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>beyond.  After the bang the sound recedes quickly.

If the aircraft is traveling faster than sound, by definition the
sound will arrive after it passes. Trailing behind is an
omnidirectional cone of noise. The higher the aircraft or bullet, the
smaller the intersection of cone and ground.

>The amount of noise in the bang depends on many different factors, such
>as overpressure generated by the lift from the wings (which is thus
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>reaches you all at once.

Yes, if it's traveling close to the speed of sound, sound is
accumulating. If it's pulling out of a dive, accumulated sound arrives
all at once. 

But objects that issue no sound (such as a bullet) can still produce
sonic booms. The act of traveling fast produces a pressure wave.

>None of this applies to bullets (but does to rockets) because they
>don't, of themselves, make noise.  They have no wings to create
>overpressure from lift, they are only a single shape so have no
>interference.  Bullets do create a 'sonic boom' but it is trivial
>compared to the unsilenced explosion that drives it.

Everyone who's been around guns knows that rifles (and artillery)
produce a 'crack' that's distinct from the explosion sound.
```

```
> They go from 7 to 30 grains.

You will also note that they use different bullets and these will be
set with different depths.  The powder is not to be left loose, the
bullet should seat on top of it.

> Unexpectedly, muzzle velocity is not linear to the load.

Well I certainly wouldn't expect it to be linear with load.  For a
start it wouldn't be linear because the force required increases as the
square of the velocity (simple physics).  Secondly different powder
types are used and these would have different expansion
characteristics.

> Trailing behind is an omnidirectional cone of noise.

You should learn what 'omni' means.

The sound does spread laterally.

> The higher the aircraft or bullet, the smaller the intersection of
cone and ground.

Where did you get that from ?  Perhaps you could explain in what way it
would be 'smaller'.  For example a Concorde leaves a sonic boom path a
few miles wide while that of a Tornado 'on the deck'  would be a few
hundred yards.

>>  Bullets do create a 'sonic boom'

> But objects that issue no sound (such as a bullet) can still produce
> sonic booms. The act of traveling fast produces a pressure wave.

Exactly. As I said.  They can also produce noise when subsonic, but
much less.

> Everyone who's been around guns knows that rifles (and artillery)
> produce a 'crack' that's distinct from the explosion sound.

Yes, but your original was:

RW>> Contrary to popular belief, a gun's bang does not come
RW>> from exploding powder; it's a sonic boom caused by the projectile.

Now, at last, you agree:

1)  there is "an explosion sound" which _is_ "a gun's bang".
2)  the 'crack' is only a part of the sound - a small part in many
cases.
3)  "Popular belief" is actually right, the "bang" does come from
exploding powder.
```

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2004-12-12T13:36:14+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<321i43F3gpl03U1@individual.net>`

```

"LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message
news:R6Hud.6314$ZP5.1939@fe40.usenetserver.com...
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
> >
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Daniel, your opinions here are as valuable as anyone else's, whatever your
stated predilection. Don't be afraid to post. Just respond as you have done
and avoid tub thumping (not that you do... but you know what I mean).

Having said that, I also believe the state has the right to kill (or it
should do). But then, I don't accept the commandment in the first place.

For a long time (based on my innate liberal tendencies) I was persuaded that
the death penalty is wrong. I accepted some of the old arguments:

1. Better for a dozen guilty men to go free than for one innocent man to be
executed.
2. There is no deterrent effect in knowing you may lose your life for
extreme anti-social behaviour.
3. Two wrongs don't make a right. (If you accept that all killing is wrong).

I changed my mind when I lived in London and saw innocent people being blown
to pieces, lives destroyed, young people who had no idea what Cromwell did
or didn't do to the Irish, having a beer and then finding their legs gone,
maimed in the pursuit of a political agenda. These "men" claimed to be
"soldiers". But my recollection of soldiering is not about cowardly murder.

It made me very angry (especially after a couple of near misses myself). I
don't know how many of you have ever been shot at, but in my case (it
happened in Bangladesh, and again in Beirut, although that was less
personal, I just happened to be in the  wrong place...),  my reaction was
not what I would have expected. I would expect to be very afraid; that isn't
what happened. All I felt was anger. "Those people are trying to kill me and
they don't even KNOW me!  Bastards!" If I had had a gun I would certainly
have returned fire with the intention of killing someone. (It is easy to see
how wars start...). The London bombings had a similar effect on me, and made
me re-think my whole consideration about capital punishment. If these guys
are such "soldiers", let's use a firing squad...

Examining the above arguments again...

1. Better for a dozen guilty men to go free than for one innocent man to be
executed.

Yes. I still believe that. There must be NO doubt that someone is guilty
before the law sanctions termination. I also believe the execution should be
painless and quick. A needle in the back of the hand saves the taxpayer a
lot of money and rids the world of someone who was totally unsuited to live
in it. (And, yes, I would take the same position if it was my nearest and
dearest or even myself.)

2. There is no deterrent effect in knowing you may lose your life for
extreme anti-social behaviour.

Maybe, for some people. Not proven. Certainly, in the old days when
punishments were harsher, crime did not stop, and people will always do
irrational things in the heat of the moment. However, there is some evidence
(based on accounts from prisoners who did not kill their victims) that the
death penalty is a deterrent to premeditated crime.

3. Two wrongs don't make a right. (If you accept that all killing is wrong).

I don't accept that all killing is wrong. Some factions on the planet are
like a cancer. We have no cure for it, other than to cut it out.

So, there are the explained views of someone who loves his fellow man, is
far from being a redneck, but who is heartily sick of the outrageous crimes
we see perpetrated by sick people and fanatics. I believe the death penalty
can be justifed for crimes of terrorism (yes, I know one man's "terrorist"
is another's "freedom fighter", but if your cause is just, pursue it
justly...) and crimes against children.

It has been interesting to me to see the attempt to reconcile a Commandment
from God with the need for punishment in a modern society.

Doc says it hinges around the word "murder". King James' translators didn't
see it that way.

If, as Doc says, the original is two words, with one of them conveying the
idea of "no", then it would be necessary to understand the original language
and the customs of the people who used it (just as it is when learning any
"foreign" language) before reaching a conclusion. There are wide differences
in meaning between "murder" and "kill".

What works for me, is to reach a conclusion based only on what I observe
going on around me and not based on any religious imperative.

Pete.
```

```
if    law-was-broken
   and guilt-proven
     perform mete-out-punishment
end-if

If it happens to have a deterrent effect, that's even better - but where 
capital punishment gives you bang for your buck is that your repeat 
offender rate is 0%!  :)

New International Version - "You shall not murder."
http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Exodus%2020:13;&version=31;

New King James Version - "You shall not murder."
http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Exodus%2020:13;&version=50;

Holman Christian Standard Bible - "Do not murder."
http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Exodus%2020:13;&version=77;

> If, as Doc says, the original is two words, with one of them conveying the
> idea of "no", then it would be necessary to understand the original language
> and the customs of the people who used it (just as it is when learning any
> "foreign" language) before reaching a conclusion. There are wide differences
> in meaning between "murder" and "kill".

Context could be of some assistance.  Exodus 20 is the Ten Commandments, 
which Moses received on the mountain.  When he came down, the people had 
created a golden calf and were worshipping it.  The Lord commanded that 
the calf be ground down, mixed in water, and those who had been 
worshipping it made to drink the water.  Many got sick, and many died. 
This happened just after He gave the order that the folks back in 1611 
translated as "Thou shalt not kill."
```

```
personal. The sanitization of war is partly responsible for the increase in
it. Any idiot can unthinkingly follow orders and push a button, destroying
dozens of people hundreds of miles away.

It was interesting to me that when we did our basic military training, we
were not required to do the famous bayonet drill (screaming and yelling as
you stab straw men to death). I asked why this was, and the response was
profound: "The NZ Army has decided that such drills are degrading to the
soldier and serve no useful purpose. If you are ever in a situation where
you need to use a bayonet, you will not require training. You will certainly
be trained in how to attach it quickly." Given that our forces seem to enjoy
a good reputation in military circles, the policy appears to have had no
deleterious effect.


<snip>
> I'm with you there.  However, I'm a bit skeptical of this "recent DNA
> evidence" stuff that anti-death-penalty folks are harping on.  I'm not
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Not sure. I am uninformed on this.
<snip>
>
> In my opinion, whether it's a deterrent or not isn't an issue.  It's a
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Well, we diverge a bit there. I see no point in punishment per se. What does
it achieve? It won't fix what was done. I see punishment ONLY as leading to
change on the part of the perpetrator, and even then, only as a last resort.
I favour the death penalty for certain crimes (as explained previously) not
for punishment, but because there is no hope of the guilty people being
reclaimed by society.  They have shown by their actions that they are not
fit to be in a civilised society and executing them is the simplest most
efficient solution. (I agree that it probably makes all concerned (except
the perp) feel better, too.)

When I manage projects I strive for a "blame free" culture. It is the same
principle. Someone goofed off; make sure they won't ever do so again, not by
punishment but by getting them to take the responsibility for the effect of
their actions. Then it becomes a learning process. It has to be "safe" to
get it wrong occasionally.

I'll amend your code as follows:

 if    law-was-broken
      and guilt-proven
           if  NOT (terrorist or paedophile)
               if NOT habitual-offender
                     if perp-accepts-responsibility AND shows-remorse
                        perform allow-perp-to-make-things-right
                        *> could be community service. MUST be equal to
gravity of crime.
                     else
                         perform mete-out-punishment
                         *> probably prison
                     end-if
               else
                     perform mete-out-max-punishment
                     *> long term prison, possibly death
               end-if
           else
               perform quick-painless-termination
           end-if
 end-if

Even this is nothing like good enough for reality. We need shades of grey in
crime and punishment, and we need to be aware of exactly what we are seeking
to achieve by punishing people.

I'd rather fix something than discard it. But when it cannot be fixed, then
it MUST be discarded.

> If it happens to have a deterrent effect, that's even better - but where
> capital punishment gives you bang for your buck is that your repeat
> offender rate is 0%!  :)
>
Lol! True.
```

```
OK, I think I have what you are saying. Your dispute is with "increase".
There have been many skirmishes around the World during the period you
elected to discuss (I did not put dates on my statement). I agree that
"increase" is vague and I was actually referring to the scale of casualties
rather than the number of conflicts. These days technology makes it possible
for accurate, efficient killing (fire and forget, smart bombs, and so on) so
the casualties in conflict are higher than they would have been in a
conflict of similar size (in terms of numbers of combatants), BEFORE these
weapons were available. (That's probably why they were developed.)

If we consider major battles in a previous era, 1,200,000 men died on the
Somme in four and a half months, the British losing 60,000 in one day. Had
they had tactical nuclear weapons the whole thing would have been over in
days as the underground German bunkers which withstood the allied barrage
would not have done so if nuclear shells had been used. You would have ended
up with around a million dead Germans (instead of 650,000) and Verdun would
have been relieved sooner.

My contention (which I did not make as clear as I should have) is that
modern weapons cause more deaths with less effort, and discomfort to the
user. This capability is partly responsible for war still being considered
an acceptable option for dispute resolution.

Pete.


>
```

```
This is very valid, Doc and I do not want to minimise the deterrent effect
of nuclear weapons. Probably the only reason that I was never required to go
to war was because of nuclear weapons.

OK, I'll really try to spell out what I want to get across (I accept that
the problem is with my expressing and not with anyone's reading.)

Modern technology makes it possible for a small group of people to destroy
large numbers of people. (I agree that this hasn't happened to any large
extent, but I hope you'd agree that the possibility is there.) Therefore if
a large group of people are equipped with modern technology, they could
potentially destroy a VERY large group of other people. They would do this
possibly without ever seeing the enemy. It is sanitised and dispassionate.
(HMS Sheffield in the Falklands crisis was destroyed by an exocet missile
launched from a Mirage below the horizon, they never saw what hit them until
it was too late; HMS Tigerfish sank the Belgrano with a torpedo that was
fired from over 25 miles away while fully submerged.)

When this kind of capability is available, there is a very strong pressure
on military commanders to use it...

I wonder if Tigerfish would have taken on the Belgrano if the battle was to
be fought with cannon...or if the mirage pilot would have been so keen
(against a modern battleship equipped with missiles) if he only had light
weapons or bombs (as in the second world war in the pacific).

The "easier" it is the more tempting it is. Nukes are an exception because
altough it only takes the synchronised turn of two keys and press of a
button, the results are very well understood by all concerned. Mutually
Assured Destruction has proven to be an effective if regrettable policy.

> >
> >My contention (which I did not make as clear as I should have) is that
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Fair enough. The deterrent effect has worked so far. But it isn't just
nuclear weapons.

> >This capability is partly responsible for war still being considered
> >an acceptable option for dispute resolution.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
I agree that we are unlikely to see the end of it. My point was that making
it "easier" doesn't help.

Pete.
```

```
In article <326brpF3ibl2hU1@individual.net>,
 "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> OK, I think I have what you are saying. Your dispute is with "increase".
> There have been many skirmishes around the World during the period you
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pete.

I see what you are saying, but I can't help but think you have it 
completely backwards.

Compare the collateral damage in the bombing of Dresden or Schweinfurt 
to the collateral damage in recent bombing Kabul or Bagdad.  Smarter, 
more powerful weapons allow troops to surgically remove whatever they 
want.  The older, dumber, weapons were akin to amputation as a 
catch-all.  The end result is less killing.

The last 60 years have seen less and less in the way of massive wars.  
The idea that any war could cause a nuclear nation to start throwing 
atomic weapons around has been a controlling and limiting factor.

There were a number of incidents between the various democracies and the 
USSR equal or greater than the murder of a minor piece of nobility in a 
backwater state.  None were ever allowed to escalate to the level of the 
Great War and battles like the Somme BECAUSE of the fear of nuclear 
weapons.

You suggest that war is not an acceptable option for dispute resolution 
-- how can you say that without knowing the alternative?
```

```
Fair enough. Maybe I have.

> Compare the collateral damage in the bombing of Dresden or Schweinfurt
> to the collateral damage in recent bombing Kabul or Bagdad.  Smarter,
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I agreed that. But I don't want to reopen this whole discussion.

> There were a number of incidents between the various democracies and the
> USSR equal or greater than the murder of a minor piece of nobility in a
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> You suggest that war is not an acceptable option for dispute resolution

> -- how can you say that without knowing the alternative?
>
Because the alternatives are well known. There are mechanisms for Conflict
Resolution (they form the underpinnings of Negotiation as a science.) There
is a whole pschology of Conflict Resolution that examines the causes and
most usual triggers of conflict. Have a look at Mayers Wheel on GOOGLE.  I
am currently preparing seminars for presentation to industry based on
exactly this, and moving on to Negotiation. Useful skills for everybody, and
particularly, middle and senior managers. I KNOW that disputes, even the
most complex ones CAN be resolved, without war. I know this because I have
been resolving disputes and negotiating for the last 25 years. I have done
some formal training in it, but most of my "expertise" in this has been
acquired "on the job".

War is simply one strategy that can be appealing where you have miltary
superiority. It saves a lot of time and the outcome is more or less
guaranteed. If you are not concerned over the likely costs in human and
resource terms, it is a useful option. The trouble is that once someone uses
it, all other parties have to use it too. (Escalation).

I am not naive or foolish enough to believe we are ever going to eliminate
it, but we can certainly ensure it is an option of last resort.

In the commmercial arena when companies go to "war" there are seldom true
"winners". That is why you have the major players forming cartels (overtly
or covertly) to protect themselves from the effect of a true market with
competition in it. Oil companies are a prime example...they pretend to be at
war but the reality is very different. Airlines too, spring immediately to
mind.

(Think about Freddy Laker... forced out of business by price cutting and as
soon as he was gone, a hike in air fares conveniently blamed on uncertainty
over oil...The major airlines concerned lost some money getting him out, but
within 6 months they had recouped it as if he had never been there. Check
out the war between Virgin and British Airways. Branston was made of
different stuff and had the resources to fight back.(Only by selling his
music business to support his airline) Nevertheless, Virgin Airlines were on
a knife edge for quite some time.)

While A and B are perceived to be overtly competing (having secretly agreed
limits that will protect them both), X, Y, and Z are forced out of business,
leaving A and B to carve the market up between themselves.

Even bullies can be persuaded to eschew force as a means of influence. (All
it takes is a greater force, or the perception of one... <G>).

I am not advocating that we should all disband our militaries (even if I
wish we could...). I am simply saying we should not be keen to use war as an
option.

Pete.


>
```

```
Top posted just to be different...

Hmmm, I agree that the various conflict resolution mechanisms are great 
for resolving disputes and should be used wherever possible.  In most 
cases they are just great.

But they suffer from a fatal flaw -- all parties to a dispute must agree 
to participate.  It does no good to have one party show up and 
decapitate the arbitrator (therapist?).

What form of conflict resolution would you suggest to resolve Osama Bin 
Laden's dispute with western democracies to both parties satisfaction?  
What middle ground is there for the FARC and the elected government of 
Columbia?  Or the christian Sudanesse and the muslim Sudanesse?

How do you negotiate a resolution when one parties absolute, 
unalterable, position is -- "those guys over there are evil and must die 
to make the world right".
```

```
> What form of conflict resolution would you suggest to resolve Osama Bin
> Laden's dispute with western democracies to both parties satisfaction?

Pay my going rate for international terrorist negotiation, and I'll tell you
<G>. (Actually, all joking aside, even this is not impossible. If Osama was
a bit tired of being PE#1 it could be possible to negotiate a cessation of
hostilities. and thence to some concessions by both sides that would be

willingness to resolve the situation on the part of all parties.

a premium for this...<G>)

> How do you negotiate a resolution when one parties absolute,
> unalterable, position is -- "those guys over there are evil and must die
> to make the world right".

You get him to accept that the "world will NEVER be right" because his
premise is impossible. (If he won't do that you have question how serious he
is in wanting resolution.) You can then start looking at how it can be made
less imperfect, for all concerned.
```
