"LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message
news:iU8vd.7062$ZP5.1891@fe40.usenetserver.com...
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
> > "LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Fair call. (It is a bit of a worry that the perimeter was poorly enough
secured to allow people to come and take potshots at the people in the
camp.)

> >>No one was shot during that incident, but he did have friends that died
> >>when the lone SCUD missile hit the barracks a few days later.
> >>
> >
> > Modern weapons require such bravery, don't they? It isn't like the old
days
> > when you had to look at the face of an enemy as you killed him, close up
and
> > personal. The sanitization of war is partly responsible for the increase
in
> > it. Any idiot can unthinkingly follow orders and push a button,
destroying
> > dozens of people hundreds of miles away.
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>
No, it isn't. It's a pity we can't find less disastrous ways to resolve our
differences, though.

I read a book many years ago by a General Hackman (British, I think) called
"The Third World War". It was written during the Cold War and he postulated
incidents occuring on the East German border, and escalating until Russian
tanks were sweeping into Europe. At some point the heavily outnumbered NATO
commander had to use Tactical Nuclear weapons, and this in turn escalated to
the use of Strategic Nuclear weapons. The interesting thing about the book
was that, unlike more conventional conflicts that go on for years, this one
could not last more than four days. The reason was that the consumption of
resources during mutual nuclear attack was such that there could be no means
of production and no infrastructure to produce more munitions, after four
days. Everything was plain wiped out.

We've come a long way since bows and arrows. (At least as far as technology
is concerned.)

> > It was interesting to me that when we did our basic military training,
we
> > were not required to do the famous bayonet drill (screaming and yelling
as
> > you stab straw men to death). I asked why this was, and the response was
> > profound: "The NZ Army has decided that such drills are degrading to the
> > soldier and serve no useful purpose. If you are ever in a situation
where
> > you need to use a bayonet, you will not require training. You will
certainly
> > be trained in how to attach it quickly." Given that our forces seem to
enjoy
> > a good reputation in military circles, the policy appears to have had no
> > deleterious effect.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Our BMT was not really stressful. There was very little hazing or abuse. I
don't recall any humiliation, either. Certainly there was drill and
discipline but that was not the nub of it. Initiative and questioning were
encouraged, and self reliance as well as teamwork were favoured. The idea
was that if your officers are dead you can still continue (this was
considered to be an edge over the Russians, who were thought to collapse if
the chain of command was broken. Fortunately the world was not required to
put this theory to the test.)

Our OC was a Kiwi who had served with the American Green Berets and our own
SAS. Nevertheless, he was a thoughtful and fair man and an excellent role
model for us.

The stress came in the difficulty of the tasks we were set. We lost three
guys and one instructor (hospitalized) during training - people really
shouldn't mess with hand grenades and I'm not sure that live fire is helpful
on a training exercise...- but by the end of it we were ready for anything.
Most of it took place in the middle of the North Island (it's a desert)
during winter (snow everywhere and bitter cold) and I seem to remember the
cold was the worst stress.  No, I just remembered, air attack was the worst.
We were attacked (having been warned it might happen at some point in the
day) from the air by aircraft you could not hear approaching (they were
supersonic - MacDonnell Douglas Skyhawks). I'll never forget that. You could
count the rivets on the buggers and your only thought was to try and be
somewhere else... Had they been strafing or dropping napalm I would not be
writing this. I remember thinking later: "The Air Force might be a good
place to be..." <G>

> >>I'm with you there.  However, I'm a bit skeptical of this "recent DNA
> >>evidence" stuff that anti-death-penalty folks are harping on.  I'm not
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Can't comment. Apart form saying that it might be wise not to taint or
illegally obtain it...

> >>In my opinion, whether it's a deterrent or not isn't an issue.  It's a
> >>defined punishment for wrong actions.  That's what most of the law is -
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> > When I manage projects I strive for a "blame free" culture. It is the
same
> > principle. Someone goofed off; make sure they won't ever do so again,
not by
> > punishment but by getting them to take the responsibility for the effect
of
> > their actions. Then it becomes a learning process. It has to be "safe"
to
> > get it wrong occasionally.
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Agreed. Anything that is downright malicious (or mean) comes in this
category.
> >
> > I'll amend your code as follows:
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Ah, but that's because we don't share a desire for "punishment", Daniel. <G>

> > Even this is nothing like good enough for reality. We need shades of
grey in
> > crime and punishment, and we need to be aware of exactly what we are
seeking
> > to achieve by punishing people.
> >
> > I'd rather fix something than discard it. But when it cannot be fixed,
then
> > it MUST be discarded.
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Hypothetical. But I'll try...

If I got 5 years in jail and knew I deserved it, I might consider that was
fair and, having "paid my debt to society" (what a pathetic phrase that
really is...), resolve to behave in future. (5 years with "Good behaviour"
(and mine would be exemplary... <G>), would be nearer three in NZ). A lot
would depend on the time inside. If I was degraded and demeaned my worldview
would probably change and I'd come out feeling "society had a debt to me". I
would then embark on the dwindling spiral that would simply cost the
taxpayer a lot more money.(I have a feeling that may have been part of the
idea in the remake of "Cape Fear" (the De Niro one)...not sure.)

I don't see "punishment" as a rehabilition tool UNLESS it involves the perp
taking responsibility for what he did and deciding that it would be better
to try and fix it. That "change of mind" should be genuine because it makes
sense, and not simply induced by the fear of punishment and the desire to
avoid it.

In my opinion, punishment is not about rehabilitation, it is about making
victims feel better. Now, I'm all in favour of victims getting compensated
by perps and all in favour of victims getting to feel better, but I'd like
it better if victims could feel better without actually needing to see
punishment.

I was talking with an elderly lady here a few days ago, who had been beaten
(some years ago) and had her house invaded by a man who was caught. He was
jailed for some time (I honestly don't know how long and I didn't ask her.)
Some time afterwards, the perp came to her house. She was terrified and
threatened to call the Police, but he simply said: "I thought about what I
did, and I am truly sorry. If you let me, I'll try and make it up to you."
He ended up painting her house for her, though she never let him inside. It
got to a point where they could sit on the verandah and have tea or coffee
together and neither of them ever spoke of it again. When the house was
done, he thanked her for helping him work it out, and she has never seen him
since.

I reckon that has to be better (for all concerned) than 50 lashes or
mutilation...

> >>Ah - another ally in the war on terror!  :)  I would also say that
> >>government is well within its rights, and is executing its duty (no pun
> >>intended), by executing those who have unjustly taken the life of
> >>another man.
> >>
I see the ultimate penalty as reserved for the "hopeless" cases, who are so
beyond the pale that society simply shouldn't have to put up with them.
(Neither should we have to support them in prison.) Executing someone who
has unjustly taken the life of another man, MIGHT qualify, but it would be
necessary to weigh each case on its merits (as most civilised countries try
to do).
> >
> > As long as due legal process is observed and it is not just an
"Executive
> > Decision". Only after we are satisfied that the accused had proper
access to
> > any and all means of defence, can we be satisfied we are behaving
properly.
> > (see my point about becoming scum in an earlier post)
>
> Well, I'll point to your example above on the ELSE leg of the NOT
> (terrorist or pedophile) condition.  Quick termination!

Sure, but NOT as a general rule, Daniel, and I did say that that contrived
COBOL "logic" is NOT suitable for Reality.
>

…[4 more quoted lines elided]…

…[17 more quoted lines elided]…

"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote in message
news:cUtvd.505325$%k.324729@pd7tw2no...

> Smaller nation - less conflict, primarily ex-Brits and Maoris - you've

This is way off the beam, Jimmy. NZ is NOT paradise (well, the bit where I
live is close, but you know what I mean...<G>).

We have problems here with unresolved treaty issues between European and
Maori. We are working on it. Maori and Europeans of goodwill are trying to
contain a lunatic fringe on both sides. It will be resolved and, hopefully,
without Civil War, or a repetition of the 1860s when Maori fought the
British. (They were never defeated, by the way. It was kind of declared a
draw, but there were many injustices and ignominies on both sides.)

Secondly, those of us who were born here do not consider ourselves ex-Brits.
We are New Zealanders and proud of it. (That is no reflection on the
British, whom I hold in high regard and affection. They were good to me
during my sojourn in their islands; I hope that those who ARE ex-Brits in
our society enjoy their stay here equally as much. Some of them are now
adopted Kiwis (like Richard) and we consider them as ourselves. Anyone who
embraces our life style and values is welcome here. Problems arise when this
doesn't happen, but the hope is in the young people and the generations born
here. They see more than their parents, sometimes.)

 As I mentioned previously, there is rich diversity here. We have MANY
different nationalities and they all contribute to contemporary Kiwi life
and culture. Yes, there are squabbles and friction sometimes, but you are
unlikely to see things like the Watts riots in L.A. or the Brixton riots in
London. It isn't our way and I hope it never becomes so; it is hard to say
it never will, as we are subject to television from England, Australia and
the States... We already have Maori Rap music...(no comment).

> Democracy is a tough grind
> as countries get bigger.
>
Yes, it is. But the necessity is to get to the kids and instil in them the
love of, need for, and appreciation of, freedom and tolerance. A "fair go"
for everybody...

Pete.

On 13-Dec-2004, "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:

> So what was your particular path Pete, and no dispute about your own
> personal morality, how did you arrive at your own set of rules to live by ?

People learn behaviors by watching people around them.   Sometimes these
behaviors are codified by religions and states.   But they don't vary a whole
lot - even though there are a bunch of religions that are "wrong".   (Yes, I
know that a there is a small minority of people who believe all religions are
"right" - but I suspect they don't think a religion is the source of their
ethical and moral values).

I can see an argument that an atheist doesn't have the security of knowing that
stoning someone is "right" - just because his book tells him to do so.   Or that
a non-patriot doesn't have the security of knowing that killing people in
another country is right - just because his king says so.

The test would be to measure what someone actually does and compare it to his
belief.   If Christians act better than Hindus or Muslims act better than
Atheists, then some conclusions can be drawn about how people develop and use
their personal morality.

```
On Mon, 13 Dec 2004 19:41:36 GMT, "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca>
wrote:

>Even accepting Richard's premise that the Israelite tales are mythical, 
>I think we could all agree that the Ten Commandments (excluding the 
>first), are a pretty wise set of  rules for any human to follow - and 
>are the most challenging set of rules for mankind. to observe. We all 
>break many of them consistently.

The authors of the US Constitution didn't think so. Many teachings of
the Ten Commandments are contradicted by modern law and culture. 

1st Commandment: "Thou shalt have no other gods before me."
3rd Commandment: "Thou shalt not take the name of the LORD thy God in
vain."

The First Amendment of the US Constitution repudiates of the First
Commandment's prohibition of religious freedom and the Third
Commandment's prohibition of freedom of speech.

The Third Commandment was not about cuss words; it was about using the
Lord's name frivolously. For example, printing it on money.

4th Commandment: "Remember the sabbath day, to keep it holy. Six days
shalt thou labour, and do all thy work: But the seventh day is the
sabbath of the LORD thy God: in it thou shalt not do any work, thou,
nor thy son, nor thy daughter, thy manservant, nor thy maidservant,
nor thy cattle, nor thy stranger that is within thy gates"

10th Commandment "Thou shalt not covet thy neighbour's house, thou
shalt not covet thy neighbour's wife, nor his manservant, nor his
maidservant, nor his ox, nor his ass, nor any thing that is thy
neighbour's." 

The Thirteenth Amendment outlaws the institution of slavery.

Wives are no longer property.

What if the stranger in your home is Jewish or Muslim? This is another
prohibition of religious freedom.

2nd Commandment: "Thou shalt not make unto thee any graven image, or
any likeness of any thing that is in heaven above, or that is in the
earth beneath, or that is in the water under the earth. Thou shalt not
bow down thyself to them, nor serve them: for I the LORD thy God am a
jealous God, visiting the iniquity of the fathers upon the children
unto the third and fourth generation of them that hate me. And shewing
mercy unto thousands of them that love me, and keep my commandments."

"Visiting the iniquity of the fathers upon the children" raises
ethical concerns. If a man murders, we don't execute his grandson.

As for graven images, pictures of Jesus are commonplace -- in churches
and paintings, even on candles. What about crosses and crucifixes? Is
every image in movies, TV and home photography "graven"?

This Commandment is yet another prohibition of religious freedom.

6th Commandment:  "Thou shalt not murder."

Does it apply to the military? What about Joshua's army marching
through Canaan, ordered by God to kill every Pagan man, woman and
child? What about witches?

7th Commandment "Thou shalt not commit adultery."

Referred to sex with a married woman, based on the precept that women
are property (see 10th Commandment above). Does apply to sex with a
single person nor one of the same gender.
```

_(reply depth: 15)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>

- **References:** `<31cge3F38hd7vU1@individual.net> <41B7372E.79818F67@mb.sympatico.ca> <10rgrqmge3d40b4@news.supernews.com> <31s5rpF3fnmhkU1@individual.net> <cpat0e$n7$1@panix5.panix.com> <31scqsF3fjvp6U1@individual.net> <R6Hud.6314$ZP5.1939@fe40.usenetserver.com> <321i43F3gpl03U1@individual.net> <cg0vd.6726$ZP5.5891@fe40.usenetserver.com> <3248o4F3fa9s6U1@individual.net> <iU8vd.7062$ZP5.1891@fe40.usenetserver.com> <Qfmvd.503093$%k.410522@pd7tw2no>`

```

"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote in message
news:Qfmvd.503093$%k.410522@pd7tw2no...
> LX-i wrote:
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> >> moral dilemmas?"  I found this hurtful in the extreme but now it
doesn't
> >> bother me. I just live my life and apply the rules I believe in.
> >
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
OK, but ONLY a little and only because you asked...

> Possibly not, but was your father an aetheist ?

No. Neither was my Mother. My Mother prayed for me every day (especially
when I was away overseas and in less safe places than Tauranga) and I don't
believe it ever did me any harm.

>Assuming he wasn't then
> regardless of whether you went to a secular public school or other,
> inevitably there in the background, as part of NZ society, back then, an
> element of 'Christianity' would have crept through.

I did go to Sunday School and I was baptised as a baby.  At 13 my father
died tragically and I was distraught (so was my Mum - we both adored him).
People were very supportive and the local vicar told me if I wanted to talk
about things he was there. I decided to try and get some answers so I went
to see him. I'm not going to repeat the discussion here, but at the end of
it, I was alarmed to realise he had no more idea about anything than I did.
He trotted out trite answers to important questions, based on rote learning
of scripture, and demonstrating very little understanding. He could accept
it was all an unknowable mystery; I couldn't.

I resolved there and then that I would find answers. First I had to decide
what the questions were, and Kipling had already pointed the way...

I keep six honest serving-men
  (They taught me all I knew);
Their names are What and Why and When
  And How and Where and Who.
I send them over land and sea,
  I send them east and west;
But after they have worked for me,
  I give them all a rest.

I let them rest from nine till five,
  For I am busy then,
As well as breakfast, lunch, and tea,
  For they are hungry men.
But different folk have different views.
  I know a person small-
She keeps ten million serving-men,
  Who get no rest at all!

She sends'em abroad on her own affairs,
  From the second she opens her eyes-
One million Hows, two million Wheres,
  And seven million Whys!

 (This is the reporter's code for newspapermen to this day).

So, over a period of around thirty years, and still continuing on some of
the questions...

Where am I?  Got seriously into Astronomy on this one. By the time I was 17
I was president of the Astronomy club at the College (Teacher Training,
Ardmore) I attended. It fascinates me to this day, and, as I have grown and
my awareness has grown, I have more understanding of it today than I ever
had. I now feel "comfortable" with the Universe and my location in it.

What am I?  Natural Sciences soon revealed the relationship between Humans
and the rest of the organisms on the planet. I also acquired respect for all
life in passing.

How am I? Well, it varies... Good days, bad days <G>. Stimulated an interest
in Philosophy and Logic which was helpful in my career.

When am I? Led me to an investigation of Time and thence to Relativity and
Riemann.

Who am I? The vicar's response had been "You are a child of God, my Son..."
Yeah, right... The search for identity is one of the most driven ones. Today
I know who I am and that also is "comfortable". I arrived there after a 25
year study of comparative religions, philosophies and cults.

I have read the Koran and the Bible,  the teachings of Lao Tze,
Zarathrustra, and Confucius, the life and teachings of the Buddha, and
Bhagavad Gita too. I have had long discussions with Scientologists and
people from Eckankar, and devotees of Guru Maha Raj Ji, and Witches,
Wizards, Freemasons, and Rosicrucians and Theosophists, Oooh and I almost
forgot the Aetherius Society. This particular investigation took me to
monasteries in the Himalayas, Churches in Europe, Temples in the Far East,
and universities in New Zealand and Europe.

I drew no conclusions, just amassed data. After a year or two of thinking
about it I arrived at the conclusions I currently hold.

The only clue you have is my acknowledged atheism. But that could change...I
might have the same catharsis Saul did on the road to Damascus... however,
my personality is not a bit like Saul's, so it is unlikely... <G>

Why am I? This is the biggie. And it is totally personal. I therefore shall
not discuss it further, other than to say that "no particular reason" is
every bit as valid as "because we are working out God's plan".

It would be wrong to assume I am saying I have all the answers (and it would
also indicate a closed mind on my part). I don't, and some of the evidence
is re-evaluated often. Some of it is indeterminate or inconclusive. But I do
have enough to let me live in this place and be comfortable about it.

I don't push it to other people because all of us must "work out our own
salvation with diligence" (the dying words of the Buddha to his friend and
disciple Ananda. Interestingly, he never claimed to be (a) God, but then he
never claimed not to be either <G>). This post would never have been made if
you hadn't actually requested it and I am unlikely to discuss these things
again.


>To wit, your love of
> literature.

Arts and literature have nothing to do with my religious belief or lack of
it. I play music and I write because I HAVE to. These are passions that have
never diminished throughout my life.

>Take phrases like Tennyson's from On Westminster Bridge,
> "Dear God the very houses seem asleep.....".  You and I both know what
> the poet is attempting to describe, even ignoring the word 'God'.
>
Yes. So?

> Even accepting Richard's premise that the Israelite tales are mythical,
> I think we could all agree that the Ten Commandments (excluding the
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Don't generalise about others, Jimmy. The 10 commandments of the Hebrew God
are indeed sensible, but so are the tenets and teachings of all of the great
teachers. And it is quite possible to arrive at similar conclusions just by
travelling and observing.

> So what was your particular path Pete, and no dispute about your own
> personal morality, how did you arrive at your own set of rules to live by
?
>

A fair question, honestly asked.

I did the above, and I also observed life going on around me (in very
different cultures). I found there are more similarities than differences
between men. There is a universality about the Human condition that makes it
possible to establish rules of conduct that will cause the least problem.
(In other words, without being pious, try and make the world a "better
place"...) It is also necessary to stop and think about things sometimes. I
have never been bored.

I have had a very full and interesting life (not saying it is over <G>, but
it is certainly entering a different phase now. I want peace and tranquility
and the adventuring is probably gone...). I have no regrets, (although there
are certainly things I could have done differently or better) and, like
Robert Louis Stevenson, when the time comes, I can "lay me down with a
will".

The belief that there is no afterlife does not frighten me. I see death as a
normal part of life and I think we should make the most of this tiny window
of awareness that is open for around 70 years... I am very happy for my
atoms to rejoin the Cosmos where they were forged.

Hope this covers it,

Pete.
```

It sems quite valid to me. There is certainly no reason to believe that
anyone else will not take liberties with what was said or written, and
whether they do so consciously or not, it is inevitable that their
interpretation will be coloured by their own life experience and
personality, if nothing else.

After some thought about this, I have decided that I ascribe to the school
that says each of us should arrive at our OWN personal belief. At least that
way we have some responsibility for what we take on board, and if we get it
"wrong" or miss the point that the writer intended, then hopefully as time
goes by, we will be moved to re-evaluate and correct what we believe,
because we will find it wanting.

In a modern society where there are not masses of uneducated illiterate
people who are incapable of evaluating things for themselves, you can
seriously ask, why should people be reliant on the words of priests and
scribes. Maybe the whole edifice of organized religion needs review (but
then, I WOULD say that... <G>).

Pete.

In article <3204u3F3enroiU1@individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:

[snip]

>It is the ones who KNOW that theirs is the only true faith who are dangerous

Oh, my Goedel is killing me... and this is KNOWN to be a tenet of the only 
true faith, right?

(for those who have less English: Goedel ('oe' being a variant spelling of 
'o-umlaut') refers to Kurt G�del's (I tried to cut-n-paste there) 
Incompleteness Theorem and has a pronounciation similar to the English 
word 'girdle', a word which, among other things, describes an undergarment 
sometimes worn by women who wish to appear thinner than they are; a few 
years ago a manufacturer of women's undergarments in the United States of 
America was touting the superiority of their product in a series of 
advertisements beginning with the catch-phrase, said by a woman whose face 
showed obvious pain, annoyance and discomfort, of 'My girdle is killing 
me'... hence, when confronted with a statement which seems to fall under 
the purview of the Incompleteness Theorem in a humorous way, e.g. 
'Everything is relative!' 'Oh... is that absolutely true?' my response 
often begins with 'Oh, my Goedel is killing me...')

(for those who have more English: a joke explained is a joke lost; see 
what happens when one tries to clarify a few words?)

DD

```

On  8-Dec-2004, Peter Lacey <lacey@mb.sympatico.ca> wrote:

> Think about what the US Constitution actually says about the right to
> bear arms, as opposed to what it's usually taken to mean, and you'll get
> my drift.

You mean:
A well-regulated militia, being necessary to the security of a free State, the
right of the people to keep and bear arms, shall not be infringed; except as to
arms which are deemed insufficiently relevant to the needs of a militia[1], or
those which are deemed excessively relevant to the needs of a militia[2]; or in
such other cases and manners as the Congress, the Legislatures of the several
States, the City Council of Bumfuque, New Jersey, or any other governmental
entity
whatsoever shall decree.

[1] E.g., sawed-off shotguns.

[2] E.g., assault rifles.

================

Congress shall make no law abridging freedom of speech or of the press, unless
such abridgement shall be necessary to prevent unauthorized persons from
influencing the outcome of an election.

Each State shall appoint, in such Manner as the Legislature thereof may direct,
or the Federal Government may order in the interests of choosing the proper
candidate, a Number of Electors.

In all criminal prosecutions, other than that of  Terrorist or Accessory
thereof, the accused shall enjoy the right to a speedy and public trial.


The powers not delegated to the United States by the Constitution, nor
prohibited by it to the States, nor being mentioned once in the same
sentence with interstate commerce, are reserved to the States
respectively, or to the people, pending Supreme Court approval.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT Science versus religion: Is compromise impossible?

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
- **Date:** 2004-12-09T22:36:44-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<10ri6g0dd7s61ec@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<31cge3F38hd7vU1@individual.net> <i-Kdnc_CI_TikizcRVn-iQ@giganews.com> <1102399619.170434.159340@c13g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <10re7uu9kotq24b@news.supernews.com>`

```

"JerryMouse" <nospam@bisusa.com> wrote in message
news:10re7uu9kotq24b@news.supernews.com...
[snip]
> The important thing to remember when dealing with the Bible (or the
> Constitution) is this: It doesn't matter what the text says - what IS
> important is what the text means. Often God (or the "Founding Fathers")
> didn't say what they meant or mean what they said.

With respect to the Constitution and Founding Fathers,
Federalist 33 < http://www.constitution.org/fed/federa33.htm >
Paragraph 6 "... If there ever should be a doubt on this head, the
credit of it will be entirely due to those reasoners who, in the
imprudent zeal of their animosity to the plan of the convention,
have labored to envelop it in a cloud calculated to obscure the
plainest and simplest truths."

As I understand these words, attributed to Alexander Hamilton,
one of the Founding Fathers, the Constitution says what it means,
plainly and simply.

While the letter, published January 2, 1788, was intended to
address anti-federalist sentiments (as explained in paragraphs
one and two), I find no clearer example of "animosity to the plan
of the convention" than President Franklin Roosevelt (in 1935?)
threatening to "pack" the Supreme Court for not passing on
(approving) the constitutionality of his social agenda.

Paragraph 6 also mentions tyrannical use of power and forced
construction, both of which may be seen to have occurred by
regarding the Constitution as consisting of "the plainest and
simplest truths."

All of the Federalist Papers are available through
< http://www.constitution.org/fed/federa00.htm >.
```
